TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM


Traffic Impact and Access Study
Cumberland Farms ­ Medway, Massachusetts


T O:            Ms. Tracey J. Roll
                Assistant Vice President of Real Estate
                Gershman Brown Crowley, Inc.
                14 Breakneck Hill Road, Suite 101
                Lincoln, RI 02865
FROM:           Jason R. Plourde, P.E., PTP
                Joseph C. Tyros
DATE:           June 14, 2013



Tighe & Bond has prepared this Traffic Impact and Access Study for a proposed 4,513
square foot Cumberland Farms with 4 fueling dispensers (8 vehicle fueling positions) to be
located on the southeast corner of the Summer Street (Route 126) and Milford Street
(Route 109) intersection in Medway, Massachusetts. The site currently consists of Medway
Garden Center, a residence at 41 Milford Street, and a residence at 39 Milford Street. As
proposed, Medway Garden Center will be reduced (11,569 square feet to 4,816 square feet)
and located on the southern portion of the property, the two residences on Milford Street
will be razed, and a Cumberland Farms will be constructed on the northern portion of the
property.   This study has been prepared to evaluate the traffic impacts and access
requirements for the proposed Cumberland Farms development.

The project site is bounded by Milford Street to the north, residences to the east, Medway
Garden Center office located at 34 Summer Street to the south, and Summer Street to the
west. Since the project site abuts Summer Street and Milford Street, both local roadways,
the project will require review through the Town of Medway approval process. The site
location in relation to the surrounding roadways is shown on the map on Figure 1.

Access to the site is currently provided via three driveways on Milford Street (i.e., one for
39 Milford Street, one for 41 Milford Street, and one for Medway Garden Center) and an
approximate 100 foot wide curb cut along Summer Street for Medway Garden Center. As
proposed, the existing Milford Street driveways for 41 Milford Street and for Medway Garden
Center will be consolidated to provide a full access driveway for the Cumberland Farms
development. The existing wide curb cut on Summer Street for Medway Garden Center is
proposed to be modified to create a more defined access for the proposed Cumberland
Farms development.

As part of the reduced Medway Garden Center project, the driveway for 34 Summer Street
is planned to provide shared access for the reduced Medway Garden Center nursery and
associated office. In addition, the driveway for 39 Milford Street is planned to be modified
to provide a truck access route for Medway Garden Center. As proposed, the Medway
Garden Center driveway on Milford Street will provide a shared access between the reduced
Medway Garden Center and the proposed Cumberland Farms development.


1 Existing Conditions
Evaluation of the traffic impacts associated with the proposed development involves the
description and quantification of existing roadway and traffic conditions in the area. Based

                                              -1-
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM



on consultation with Town of Medway officials, the scope of work of the analysis was
established and the individual intersections studied as part of this analysis were determined.
In preparing this study for the proposed development, the following intersections have been
analyzed and evaluated:

   ·   Summer Street (Route 126) at Milford Street (Route 109)
   ·   Summer Street (Route 126) at Rustic Road and Medway Garden Center driveway
   ·   Milford Street (Route 109) at Medway Garden Center driveway

A description of each roadway and the geometric and traffic control characteristics of each
of these intersections are provided, followed by quantification of existing traffic volumes,
accident history, vehicle speeds, and sight distance.

1.1 Study Area Intersections
1.1.1 Summer Street (Route 126) at Milford Street (Route 109)
Summer Street and Milford Street intersect to form a four-way signalized intersection. The
Summer Street northbound and southbound approaches each consist of a single general
purpose travel lane. The Milford Street eastbound approach consists of an exclusive left-
turn lane and a shared through/right-turn lane. The Milford Street westbound approach
consists of a single general purpose travel lane. Directional flow along Summer Street and
Milford Street are separated by double yellow centerlines. The speed limit along the
Summer Street northbound and southbound approaches is posted at 30 miles per hour
(mph). The speed limit along the Milford Street eastbound and westbound approaches is
posted at 35 mph. The traffic signal operates on a four-phase cycle with a Milford Street
eastbound lead phase, a Milford Street eastbound and westbound permissive phase, a
Summer Street northbound and southbound permissive phase, and an exclusive pedestrian
phase called. Land use in the vicinity of the intersection consists of Medway Garden Center,
Restaurant 45, Medway Fire Department, and Dunkin' Donuts.

1.1.2 Summer Street (Route 126) at Rustic Road
Rustic Road and a curb cut for Medway Garden Center intersect Summer Street from the
west and east, respectively, to form a four-way unsignalized intersection, with the Rustic
Road and Medway Garden Center approaches operating under STOP control. The Summer
Street northbound and southbound approaches each consist of a single general purpose
travel lane with directional flow separated by double yellow centerlines. The Rustic Road
allows for both eastbound and westbound travel with no pavement marking separating
directional flow. Land use in the vicinity of the intersection consists of Medway Garden
Center, Restaurant 45, and residences.

1.1.3 Milford Street (Route 109) at Medway Garden Center
A curb cut for Medway Garden Center intersects Milford Street from the south to form a T-
type unsignalized intersection, with the Medway Garden Center approach operating under
STOP control. The Milford Summer Street eastbound and westbound approaches each
consist of a single general purpose travel lane with directional flow separated by double
yellow centerlines. Land use in the vicinity of the intersection consists of Medway Garden
Center, Dunkin' Donuts, and residences.

1.2 Traffic Volumes
Base traffic conditions within the study area were developed by conducting manual turning
movement counts (TMCs), vehicle classification counts, and automatic traffic recorder (ATR)
counts in May 2013. The TMCs and vehicle classification counts were performed at the
study area intersection locations during the weekday morning peak period (7:00 to 9:00
AM), the weekday afternoon peak period (4:00 to 6:00 PM), and the Saturday midday peak
period (11:00 AM to 2:00 PM). The ATR counts were conducted along Summer Street south
of Milford Street and along Milford Street east of Summer Street to collect both weekday
and Saturday daily traffic volumes and vehicle speed measurements. The traffic count data
are provided in the Appendix.

Traffic on a given roadway typically fluctuates throughout the year depending on the area
and the type of roadway. To determine if the traffic-count data needed to be adjusted to
account for this fluctuation, seasonal traffic-volume data were reviewed from a nearby
Massachusetts Department of Transportation (MassDOT) Permanent Count Station. 1 This
information revealed May traffic volumes are approximately 3.5 percent higher than annual
average-month conditions. Therefore, the May traffic counts were used as collected to
provide a conservative analytical framework (higher than average). The MassDOT seasonal
adjustment data are provided in the Appendix.

Table 1 summarizes the existing daily and peak-hour traffic volumes along Summer Street
and along Milford Street adjacent to the site. Figure 2 graphically depicts the 2013 Existing
weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday peak-hour traffic flow
networks.

1.3 Vehicle Crash Analysis
Crash data for the study area intersections were obtained from MassDOT for the most
recent three-year period available (between 2008 and 2010). In addition to the collision
summary, incident occurrence should be compared to the volume of traffic through a
particular intersection to determine significance.     Accordingly, the crash rates were
calculated for each study area intersection and compared with the statewide and district-
wide (MassDOT District 3) averages. An intersection crash rate is a measure of the
frequency of collisions compared to the volume of traffic through an intersection and is
presented in crashes per million entering vehicles (c/mev). For unsignalized intersections,
the statewide average is 0.60 c/mev and the district-wide average is 0.66 c/mev. For
signalized intersections, the statewide average is 0.80 c/mev and the district-wide average
is 0.89 c/mev. A comparison of the calculated crash rate to these averages can be used to
establish the significance of collision occurrence and whether or not potential safety
problems exist. The crash rate worksheets are provided in the Appendix. A summary of the
MassDOT crash data at the study area intersections is provided in Table 2.

The intersection of Summer Street and Milford Street has experienced 12 reported collisions
over the 3 year period with an average of 4 incidents per year. Based on this information,
the crash rate was determined to be of 0.54, which is less than the district-wide and
statewide averages for signalized intersections. Approximately 58 percent (7 of 12) of the
incidents involved property damage only, approximately 33 percent (4 of 12) were identified
as angle-type, and 33 percent (4 of 12) were identified as rear-end-type collisions. Based
on the collision history data, there does not appear to be a safety concern at this location
and there does not appear to be a particular collision pattern correctable by engineering
measures.




                                             -5-
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM



There were no collisions reported at the other study area intersections during the time
period studied. There were no fatalities reported at the study area intersections during the
time period evaluated.

1.4 Vehicle Speeds
Speed measurements were conducted along Summer Street and along Milford Street. The
travel time was recorded using ATRs over a 24-hour period, thereby also recording travel
speeds during non-peak hours when vehicle speeds are not affected by platooning. The
speeds were determined by dividing the elapsed time by the measured distance between
two checkpoints. The results of the speed measurements are summarized in Table 3.

TABLE 3
                                 a
Observed Travel Speeds

south of Milford Street:
   Northbound                              30              24                 32
   Southbound                              30              31                 38

east of Summer Street:
    Eastbound                              35              30                 38
    Westbound                              35              19                 29
a
    In miles per hour (mph).
b

As shown, the average speeds along Summer Street adjacent to the site were found to be
between 24 and 31 mph with 85th percentile speeds between 32 and 38 mph for northbound
and southbound travel. The speeds along Summer Street northbound were found to be
generally lower as vehicles were approaching the signalized intersection with Milford Street.
The speeds along Summer Street southbound were found to be generally higher as the
posted speed limit south of the study area increased from 30 mph to 40 mph. These speed
measurements were observed to be generally consistent with the posted speed limits along
Summer Street, are not excessive, and are representative of the roadway classification and
alignment.

The average speeds along Milford Street adjacent to the site were found to be between 19
and 30 mph with 85th percentile speeds between 29 and 38 mph for eastbound and
westbound travel. The speeds along Milford Street westbound were found to be generally
lower as vehicles were approaching the signalized intersection with Milford Street. These
observed speeds are not excessive and are representative of the roadway classification and
alignment. These speed measurements were observed to be generally consistent with the
posted speed limits along Milford Street, are not excessive, and are representative of the
roadway classification and alignment.


2 Future No-Build Build Conditions
To estimate the impact of site-generated traffic within the study area, existing traffic
volumes were projected to the year 2018 which represents a five-year design horizon in
accordance with MassDOT and Town of Medway traffic impact assessment guidelines. The
proposed development is expected to be completed and operational before the 2018 design


                                                           -6-
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM



year. Traffic volumes on the roadway network will include existing traffic, new traffic due to
normal traffic growth, and traffic related to significant development by others expected to
be completed within the area by 2018. Consideration of these factors resulted in the
development of 2018 No-Build traffic volumes, which assume the proposed development is
not built. The incremental impacts of the proposed development may then be determined
by adding site-generated traffic volumes (Build conditions) and making comparisons to the
No-Build conditions.

2.1 Traffic Growth
To develop the 2018 No-Build forecast volumes, two components of traffic growth were
considered. First, an annual average traffic-growth percentage was determined based on
MassDOT historical traffic-volume data.      The MassDOT historical traffic-volume data
revealed a decrease in traffic between 2004 and 2009. Such a decline in traffic, however,
may not continue. Therefore, to provide a conservative (worse than expected) basis for the
analysis, a 1.0 percent compounded annual growth rate was assumed to account for general
population growth and the traffic generated by smaller area developments.

Second, traffic to be generated by planned developments estimated to add substantial
volumes of traffic through the study area within the next five years was considered. Based
on discussions with officials from the Towns of Medway and Milford, two types of
developments were identified.      The first project involves the construction of smaller
residential developments contemplated along Summer Street north of Milford Street in
Medway. Discussions with Medway officials revealed the residential developments are not
expected to generate a significant amount of traffic within the study area and would be
accounted for within the annual background growth rate.

Based on discussions with Milford officials, there is a potential resort/casino being
considered on East Main Street (Route 16) northeast of Interstate 495 (I-495) in Milford.
Discussions with Milford officials revealed this project is currently in the preliminary planning
stages and there have not been definitive plans or documents submitted to the Town.
Therefore, the resort/casino development was not included as a background development.
If and when the development proceeds through the permitting process, a separate traffic
analysis will likely incorporate the proposed Cumberland Farms development as a
background project.

The 2018 No-Build peak-hour traffic volumes were accordingly developed by applying a 1.0
percent per year traffic growth rate (or 5.1 percent compounded over five years) to the
2013 Existing volumes. The 2018 No-Build traffic volumes are shown graphically for the
study area intersections on Figure 3 for the weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and
Saturday midday peak hours.

2.2 Planned Roadway Improvements
Based on discussions with officials from the Town of Medway, there are no planned roadway
improvements contemplated in the surrounding area of the proposed project.


3 Build Conditions
The site currently consists of Medway Garden Center and residences located at 39 and 41
Milford Street. As proposed, Medway Garden Center will be reduced to 4,816 square feet
and located on the southern portion of the property, the two residences on Milford Street
(39 and 41 Milford Street) will be razed, and a 4,513 square foot Cumberland Farms with 4
fueling dispensers (8 vehicle fueling positions) will be constructed.

3.1 Trip Generation
3.1.1 Existing Site Generation
The project site currently consists of the 11,569 square foot Medway Garden Center. To
estimate the volume of traffic to be generated by the reduced Medway Garden Center
(4,816 square feet), a local trip-generation rate was developed based on the traffic counts
conducted at the site driveways and the size of the existing floor area. Consistent with the
trip-generation methodology published by Institute of Transportation Engineers (ITE) 2 for a
Nursery (Garden Center), the outside storage areas were not included in the overall gross
floor area measurements unless they were located within the principal outside faces of the
exterior walls. The existing and projected site-generation characteristics of Medway Garden
Center are summarized in Table 4.

Based on the local trip-generation information provided in Table 4, the amount of trips
expected to be eliminated with the reduction of Medway Garden Center are 5 vehicle trips
during the weekday morning peak hour (3 entering and 2 exiting), 21 vehicle trips during
the weekday afternoon peak hour (10 entering and 11 exiting), and 84 vehicle trips during
the Saturday midday peak hour (46 entering and 38 exiting).

3.1.2 Proposed Site Generation Comparison
To estimate the volume of traffic to be generated by the proposed Cumberland Farms
development, trip-generation rates published by ITE were researched for Land Use Code
853 (Convenience Market with Gasoline Pumps). Based on data published by ITE, the

majority of vehicular trips associated with facilities containing a convenience store and
gasoline pumps are classified as impulse trips (i.e., vehicles already present in the adjacent
roadway system). Therefore, there is a better correlation of trips at these types of facilities
with the volume of adjacent street traffic than with the size of the convenience store. As a
result, the expected trips associated with the proposed Cumberland Farms development
were based on the volume of adjacent street traffic during the weekday morning and
afternoon peak hours. Since the ITE Trip Generation manual does not provide trip-
generation data for this land use during the Saturday midday peak hour based on the
volume of adjacent street traffic, the trip-generation estimates for this time period were
developed based on the number of vehicle fueling positions as the proposed facility is
proposed to be more similar to the average number of fueling positions than the average
size of the convenience store provided by ITE.

3.1.3 Proposed Site Generation Characteristics
Not all of the vehicle trips expected to be generated by the proposed Cumberland Farms
development represent new trips on the study area roadway system. A substantial portion
of the vehicles visiting commercial/retail developments have been found to already be
present in the adjacent passing traffic stream or are diverted from another route to the
subject site. Based on data presented in the ITE Trip Generation Handbook, 3 the average
pass-by trip percentage for Land Use Code 853 (Convenience Market with Gasoline Pumps)
is 63 percent during the weekday morning peak hour and 66 percent during the weekday
afternoon peak hour. To be consistent with State of Massachusetts guidelines for the
preparation of traffic impact studies and to provide a conservative (worse than expected)
basis for the analysis, however, only 25 percent of the site-generated traffic for the
proposed Cumberland Farms development was considered pass-by traffic.            Table 6
summarizes the expected peak-hour trip-generation characteristics of the proposed
development.

3.2 Arrival/Departure Distribution
After project-generated vehicle trips have been estimated, the next step in the traffic study
is to determine the distribution of project traffic and assign these trips to the local roadway
network. The directional distribution of new site traffic is dependent on the combination of
a number of factors, including: existing travel patterns, site access routes, and competing
shopping opportunities (i.e., locations that include either fueling stations and/or
convenience stores). The directional distribution of pass-by traffic was determined based on
the existing travel patterns observed along Summer Street and Milford Street adjacent to
the site as part of the TMCs, vehicle classification counts, and ATR counts in May 2013.

Based on the traffic-generation and distribution estimates for the proposed project, the
traffic volumes associated with the Cumberland Farms development were assigned to the
local roadway network. In addition, the existing trips associated with the portion of Medway
Garden Center proposed to be reduced (to remain) were redistributed along the adjacent
roadway network and the trips associated with the portion of Medway Garden Center to be
removed were subtracted from the roadway network.

The project-generated traffic volumes are shown on Figures 4 through 6 for the weekday
morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday peak hours, respectively. The project-
generated traffic volumes were assigned to the roadway network and combined with the
2018 No-Build traffic volumes to develop the 2018 Build peak-hour traffic-volume networks.
The 2018 Build weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday traffic volumes
are illustrated on Figure 7.

3.3 Site Access
Access to the site is currently provided via three driveways on Milford Street and an
approximate 100 foot wide curb cut along Summer Street. As proposed, two of the existing
Milford Street driveways will be consolidated to provide a full access driveway for the
Cumberland Farms development. The existing curb cut on Summer Street is proposed to be
modified to create a more defined access for the proposed Cumberland Farms development.
As part of the reduced Medway Garden Center project, the existing driveway for 34 Summer
Street is planned to provide shared access for the reduced Medway Garden Center nursery
and associated office. In addition, the driveway for 39 Milford Street is planned to be
modified to provide a truck access route for Medway Garden Center. As proposed, the
Medway Garden Center driveway on Milford Street will provide a shared access between the
reduced Medway Garden Center and the proposed Cumberland Farms development.

To identify potential safety concerns associated with site access and egress, sight distances
have been evaluated at the proposed Cumberland Farms site driveway locations to
determine if the available sight distances for vehicles exiting the site meet or exceed the
minimum distances required for approaching vehicles to safely stop. The available sight
distances were compared with minimum requirements, as established by the American
Association of State Highway and Transportation Officials (AASHTO) 4. AASHTO is the
national standard by which vehicle sight distance is calculated, measured, and reported. As




4A Policy on Geometric Design of Highways and Streets; American Association of State Highway and
  Transportation Officials (AASHTO); 2004.

                                               -11-
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM



stated in their guidelines for traffic impact assessments, the Massachusetts Executive Office
of Transportation (EOT) and the Executive Office of Environmental Affairs (EOEA) require
the use of AASHTO sight distance standards when preparing traffic impact assessments and
studies.

Sight distance is the length of roadway ahead visible to the driver. The Stopping Sight
Distance (SSD) is the minimum distance required for a vehicle traveling at a certain speed
to safely stop before reaching a stationary object in its path. The SSD is measured from an
eye height of 3.5 feet to an object height of 2 feet above street level, equivalent to the
taillight height of a passenger car. The SSD is measured along the centerline of the
traveled way of the major road. The Intersection Sight Distance (ISD) is provided on minor
street approaches to allow the drivers of stopped vehicles a sufficient view of the major
roadway to decide when to enter the major roadway. The ISD is measured from an eye
height of 3.5 feet to an object height of 3.5 feet above the roadway level.

The SSD is generally more important as it represents the minimum distance required for
safe stopping while the ISD is based only upon acceptable speed reductions to the
approaching traffic stream. The ISD, however, must be equal to or greater than the
minimum required SSD in order to provide safe operations at the intersection.               In
accordance with the AASHTO manual, "If the available sight distance for an entering or
crossing vehicle is at least equal to the appropriate stopping sight distance for the major
road, then drivers have sufficient sight distance to anticipate and avoid collisions. However,
in some cases, this may require a major-road vehicle to stop or slow to accommodate the
maneuver by a minor-road vehicle. To enhance traffic operations, intersection sight
distances that exceed stopping sight distances are desirable along the major road."
Accordingly, the ISD should be at least equal to the distance required to allow a driver
approaching the minor roadway to safely stop.

The available SSD and ISD at the proposed site driveway locations were measured and
compared to minimum requirements as established by AASHTO.            Since the distance
required to stop a vehicle is dependent on the speed of the vehicle, speed studies were
conducted as presented in Section 1.4 Vehicle Speeds. Based on the posted speed limit and
the observed speeds, the SSD and ISD requirements at the two proposed site driveways
were calculated. The required minimum sight distances for each speed are compared to the
available distances, as shown in Table 7.

As indicated in Table 7, available sight distances at the proposed site driveways exceed the
minimum and desirable SSD and ISD requirements for safe operation. Although sight
distances are limited to the east of the proposed Milford Street site driveway due to the
vertical roadway alignment, AASHTO sight distances requirements are achieved. To provide
the safe and efficient flow of traffic to and from the site, proposed plantings, vegetation,
landscaping, and signing along the site frontage are recommended to be kept low to the
ground or set back sufficiently from the edge of the roadways so as not to inhibit the
available sight lines.

  the posted speed limit (35 mph).
c
  Values based on SSD requirements for vehicles driving at 85th percentile speeds for Summer Street and for Milford Street west of
  site driveway. Value for Milford Street east of the site driveway is based on posted speed limit since the 85th percentile speed
  (29 mph) was observed to be lower than the posted speed limit (35 mph).




                                                                            -13-
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM




3.4 Traffic Increases
Development of the proposed project will result in increases in traffic on study area
roadways. As shown on Figure 4, the traffic-volume increases beyond the study area are
expected to be in the range of 3 to 33 vehicles during the critical time periods. These
increases represent, on average, 1 additional vehicle per 1.8 to 20 minutes.

These projected increases in traffic are based on a conservative (lower than expected) pass-
by rate of 25 percent as compared to the higher rates published by ITE from existing similar
facilities. With the lower pass-by rate, there would be more new trips to the study area
roadways beyond the site driveways than expected. Therefore, the impacts to the adjacent
roadway system beyond the site driveways are anticipated to be better than as evaluated.


4 Capacity and Queue Length Analyses
Capacity analyses were performed for the study intersections with the 2013 Existing, 2018
No-Build, and 2018 Build Condition traffic volumes during the weekday morning, weekday
afternoon, and Saturday midday peak hours using Trafficware Synchro Software. The
analysis results are categorized in terms of Level of Service (LOS), which describes the
qualitative intersection operational conditions based on the calculated average delay per
vehicle. A definition of LOS is provided in the Appendix.

Vehicle queue analyses were computed for the study intersections for the 2013 Existing,
2018 No-Build, and 2018 Build Condition traffic volumes during the weekday morning,
weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday peak hours using Trafficware Synchro Software.
The quantitative measure of vehicle queue length is defined as the 50th and 95th percentile
queue. The 50th percentile queue represents the average queue length during peak hours
and the 95th percentile queue represents the calculated maximum back of queue with the
95th percentile traffic volumes.

The capacity and queue length analysis results are discussed below and are summarized in
Table 8. The computer-generated analysis reports are provided in the Appendix.

4.1 Summer Street at Milford Street
Under existing and future traffic-volume conditions, the signalized intersection of Summer
Street and Milford Street operates at desirable levels (LOS C or better) with the lane groups
operating at LOS D or better during the weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and
Saturday midday peak hours. With the addition of the proposed development's traffic under
2018 Build Condition traffic volumes as compared to 2018 No-Build Condition traffic
volumes, the lane groups are projected to remain operating at the same service levels,
queue in the lane groups are not expected to increase by more than one vehicle, and ample
capacity will remain as evident of the volume-to-capacity (v/c) ratios less than 1.00.
Therefore, the additional traffic generated by the proposed project is expected to have
minimal impact on the operations of this intersection.

4.2 Summer Street at Rustic Road and Site Driveway
Under existing and future traffic-volume conditions, the Rustic Road and site driveway minor
street approaches to the unsignalized intersection with Summer Street operate at desirable
levels (LOS C or better) with the Summer Street main street critical movements operating
at optimal levels (LOS A) during the weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday
midday peak hours. With the addition of the proposed development's traffic under 2018
Build Condition traffic volumes as compared to 2018 No-Build Condition traffic volumes,
there are anticipated to be insignificant impacts at this intersection as there will be
increases in delay on the minor street movements of 4.5 seconds or less, the queues on the
lane group movements are expected to increase by less than one vehicle, and ample
capacity will remain as evident of the low v/c ratios during the time periods evaluated.

4.3 Milford Street at Site Driveway
Under existing and future traffic-volume conditions, the site driveway minor street approach
to the unsignalized intersection with Milford Street operates at desirable levels (LOS C or
better) with the Milford Street main street left turns operating at optimal levels (LOS A)
during the weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday peak hours. With
the addition of the proposed development's traffic under 2018 Build Condition traffic
volumes as compared to 2018 No-Build Condition traffic volumes, there are anticipated to
be insignificant impacts at this intersection as there will be increases in delay on the minor
street movements of 3.8 seconds or less, the queue on the lane group movements are
expected to increase by less than one vehicle, and ample capacity will remain as evident of
the low v/c ratios during the time periods evaluated.


5 Conclusions
Existing and future conditions in the study area have been described, analyzed, and
evaluated with respect to traffic operations and the impact of the proposed Cumberland
Farms development. Conclusions of these efforts are summarized in the following:

   ·   The site currently consists of Medway Florist and Garden Center. As proposed,
       Medway Florist and Garden Center will be reduced and located on the southern end
       of the property. In addition, a 4,513 square foot Cumberland Farms convenience
       store with 4 fueling dispensers (8 vehicle fueling positions) will be constructed on the
       northern end of the property.

   ·   As proposed, the existing driveways will be modified to create more defined access
       points. A new driveway will be provided for Medway Garden Center at the eastern
       end of the property along Milford Street and will provide shared access with the
       proposed Cumberland Farms development.

   ·   Access is currently provided to Medway Garden Center via three driveways on Milford
       Street and an approximate 100 foot wide curb cut along Summer Street. As
       proposed, two of the existing Milford Street driveways will be consolidated to provide
       a full access driveway for the Cumberland Farms development. The existing curb cut
       on Summer Street is proposed to be modified to create a more defined access for the
       proposed Cumberland Farms development. In addition, the driveway for 39 Milford
       Street is planned to be modified to provide a truck access route for Medway Garden
       Center and a shared access between Medway Garden Center and the proposed
       Cumberland Farms development.




                                            -17-
TECHNICAL MEMORANDUM



     ·    The proposed Cumberland Farms development is expected to generate 110 new
          vehicle trips (56 entering and 54 exiting) during the weekday morning peak hour,
          100 new vehicle trips (50 entering and 50 exiting) during the weekday afternoon
          peak hour, and 60 new vehicle trips (31 entering and 29 exiting) during the Saturday
          midday peak hour. During the Saturday midday peak hour, the existing portion of
          Medway Garden Center to be removed is anticipated to generate more total trips
          than the proposed Cumberland Farms development.

     ·    The additional traffic projected to be generated during the weekday morning and
          afternoon peak hours represents an increase in traffic leading beyond the study area
          of approximately 1 additional vehicle per 1.8 to 20 minutes. These traffic-volume
          increases are based on a conservative (lower than expected) pass-by rate of 25
          percent, necessitated by state guidelines. As a result, the lower pass-by rate
          produces more new trips to the study area roadways leading beyond the site
          driveways.   Therefore, proposed development could have less impacts on the
          adjacent roadway system beyond the site driveways than as analyzed.

     ·    Under existing and future traffic-volume conditions, the signalized intersection of
          Summer Street and Milford Street operates at LOS C or better during the weekday
          morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday peak hours. With the addition of
          the proposed development's traffic, the lane groups are projected to remain
          operating at the same service levels, vehicle queuing is not expected to increase by
          more than one vehicle, and ample capacity will remain as evident of the v/c ratios
          less than 1.00. Therefore, the additional traffic generated by the proposed project is
          expected to have minimal impact on the operations of this intersection.

     ·    Under existing and future traffic-volume conditions, the Rustic Road and site
          driveway minor street approaches to the unsignalized intersection with Summer
          Street operate at LOS C or better with the Summer Street main street critical
          movements operating at LOS A during the weekday morning, weekday afternoon,
          and Saturday midday peak hours. With the addition of the proposed development's
          traffic, there are anticipated to be negligible impacts at this intersection as there will
          be increases in delay on the minor street movements of 4.5 seconds or less, queues
          in the lane group movements are expected to increase by less than one vehicle, and
          ample capacity will remain as evident of the low v/c ratios during the time periods
          evaluated.

     ·    Under existing and future traffic-volume conditions, the site driveway minor street
          approach to the unsignalized intersection with Milford Street operates at LOS C or
          better with the Milford Street main street left turns operating at LOS A during the
          weekday morning, weekday afternoon, and Saturday midday peak hours. With the
          addition of the proposed development's traffic, there are anticipated to be
          insignificant impacts at this intersection as there will be increases in delay on the
          minor street movements of 3.8 seconds or less, queues in the lane group
          movements are expected to increase by less than one vehicle, and ample capacity
          will remain as evident of the low v/c ratios during the time periods evaluated.

Capacity Analysis Methodology




                     Tighe&Bond
                                                                               Tighe&Bond



                                 LEVELS OF SERVICE

Published by the Transportation Research Board, the Highway Capacity Manual (2010)
contains concepts, guidelines, and computational procedures for evaluating operational
measures and qualities of service for multi-modes of transportation facilities (e.g., roadways
and intersections) under different traffic-flow conditions. The concept of level of service
(LOS) is defined as a qualitative measure describing operational conditions within a traffic
stream and the perception by motorists and/or passengers. A level-of-service definition
provides an index to quality of traffic flow in terms of such factors as speed, travel time,
freedom to maneuver, traffic interruptions, comfort, convenience, and safety.


SIGNALIZED INTERSECTIONS

Definition

The methodology for signalized intersections assesses the effects of traffic signal type,
timing, phasing, progression; vehicle mix; and geometrics on average control delay.
Control delay at signalized intersections is associated with the time spent for motorists to
reduce speed or stop at a control signal (i.e., initial time spent decelerating, time spent
stopped at the conflict point, time spent moving with the vehicular queue, and time required
to accelerate to desired speed).        Control delay is used to establish the operating
characteristics for an intersection or an approach to an intersection. Specifically, level-of-
service criteria are stated in terms of the average control delay per vehicle for a 15-minute
analysis period. Volume-to-capacity (v/c) ratios are also used to help signify the utilization
of a lane group's capacity at an intersection. A v/c ratio of 1.00 represents conditions
when the traffic signal cycle capacity is fully utilized and indicates a capacity failure. The
criteria are given in the table below.


TABLE A-1
Level-of-Service Criteria ­ Signalized Intersections

                       Average Control Delay
 Level of Service                                      V/C Ratio >1.00
                       (seconds per vehicle)

          A                      0 ­ 10                       F

          B                    >10 ­ 20                       F

          C                    >20 ­ 35                       F

          D                    >35 ­ 55                       F

          E                    >55 ­ 80                       F

          F                       >80                         F

Source: HCM2010: Highway Capacity Manual. Washington, D.C.: Transportation
       Research Board, 2010. Page 18-6.
                                                                                                                Tighe&Bond



UNSIGNALIZED INTERSECTIONS

Definition

The level of service criteria for unsignalized intersections is based on the control delay for
each minor street movement (or shared movement) and the major street left-turn
movement. The procedure accounts for lane configuration on both the minor and major
street approaches, conflicting traffic stream volumes, and the type of intersection control
(STOP, YIELD, or all-way STOP control). Control delay at an unsignalized intersection is
defined as the total elapsed time from when a vehicle stops at the end of the queue until
the vehicle departs from the stop line. This time includes the time required for the vehicle
to travel from the last-in-queue position to the first-in-queue position.

Volume-to-capacity (v/c) ratios are also used to help signify the utilization of a movement's
capacity at an intersection. A v/c ratio of 1.00 represents conditions when the movement
is fully utilized and indicates a capacity failure. The capacity of the movements is based on
the distribution of gaps in the major street traffic stream, the selection of gaps to complete
the desired movement, and the follow-up headways for each driver in the queue. When an
unsignalized intersection is located within 0.25 miles of a signalized intersection, traffic
flows may not be random and some platoon structure may exist, thereby affecting the
minor street operations. The criteria are given in the table below.


TABLE A-2
Level-of-Service Criteria ­
Two-Way Stop-Controlled and All-Way Stop-Controlled Intersections

                                Average Control Delay
  Level of Service                                                      V/C Ratio >1.00
                                 (seconds per vehicle)

             A                               0 ­ 10                                 F

             B                             >10 ­ 15                                 F

             C                             >15 ­ 25                                 F

             D                             >25 ­ 35                                 F

              E                            >35 ­ 50                                 F

              F                                >50                                  F

Source: HCM2010: Highway Capacity Manual. Washington, D.C.: Transportation
       Research Board, 2010. Pages 19-2 and 20-3.
